mw-body-content">\n</div>\n<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Chinese space program</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Space program of the People\'s Republic of China</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the Chinese space program in general. For the current Chinese space agency since 1993, see <a href="/wiki/China_National_Space_Administration" title="China National Space Administration">China National Space Administration</a>.</div>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Earth_and_the_Moon_from_Chang%27e_5_T1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Earth_and_the_Moon_from_Chang%27e_5_T1.jpg/300px-Earth_and_the_Moon_from_Chang%27e_5_T1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Earth_and_the_Moon_from_Chang%27e_5_T1.jpg/450px-Earth_and_the_Moon_from_Chang%27e_5_T1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Earth_and_the_Moon_from_Chang%27e_5_T1.jpg/600px-Earth_and_the_Moon_from_Chang%27e_5_T1.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Earth_and_the_Moon_from_Chang%27e_5_T1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Chang%27e_5-T1" title="Chang\'e 5-T1">Chang\'e 5-T1</a> test vehicle service module took this photo of Earth and the Moon together on November 9, 2014</div></div></div>\n<p>The <b>space program of the People\'s Republic of China</b> is directed by the <a href="/wiki/China_National_Space_Administration" title="China National Space Administration">China National Space Administration</a> (CNSA). Its technological roots can be traced back to the late 1950s, when <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> began a ballistic missile program in response to perceived <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">American</a> (and, later, <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet</a>) threats. However, the first Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manned_space_flight" title="Manned space flight">crewed space</a> program only began several decades later, when an accelerated program of technological development culminated in <a href="/wiki/Yang_Liwei" title="Yang Liwei">Yang Liwei</a>\'s successful 2003 flight aboard <a href="/wiki/Shenzhou_5" title="Shenzhou 5">Shenzhou&#160;5</a>. This achievement made China the third country to independently send humans into space. Plans currently include a permanent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_space_station" title="Chinese space station">Chinese space station</a> in 2020 and crewed expeditions to the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>.\n</p><p>Officials have articulated long term ambitions to exploit Earth-Moon space for industrial development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-2">[2]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" geography">Geography</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_printing_in_East_Asia" title="History of printing in East Asia">Printing</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Chinese_ceramics" title="Chinese ceramics">Ceramics</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_metallurgy_in_China" title="History of metallurgy in China">Metallurgy</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Chinese_coinage" title="Ancient Chinese coinage">Coinage</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Chinese_alchemy" title="Chinese alchemy">Alchemy</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">Traditional medicine</a>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Chinese_herbology" title="Chinese herbology">herbology</a></li></ul></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_China" title="Agriculture in China">Agriculture</a>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Sericulture" title="Sericulture">sericulture</a></li></ul></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Silk_industry_in_China" title="Silk industry in China">Silk industry</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Chinese_architecture" title="Chinese architecture">Architecture</a>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Chinese_garden" title="Chinese garden">classic gardens</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_bridges_in_China" title="List of bridges in China">bridges</a></li></ul></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_transport_in_China" title="History of transport in China">Transport</a>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Naval_history_of_China" title="Naval history of China">navigation</a></li></ul></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Military_history_of_China_before_1911" title="Military history of China before 1911">Military</a>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Naval_history_of_China" title="Naval history of China">naval</a></li></ul></li></ul></td>\n</tr><tr><th style="padding:0.1em;background: #cb4154;;padding-top: 0; color: white;">\nBy era</th></tr><tr><td class="hlist" style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em;padding-top: 0.2em;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Science_and_technology_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="Science and technology of the Han dynasty">Han</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Science_and_technology_of_the_Tang_dynasty" title="Science and technology of the Tang dynasty">Tang</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Science_and_technology_of_the_Song_dynasty" title="Science and technology of the Song dynasty">Song</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Science_and_technology_of_the_Yuan_dynasty" title="Science and technology of the Yuan dynasty">Yuan</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Science_and_technology_in_China" title="Science and technology in China">People\'s Republic</a>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/History_of_agriculture_in_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="History of agriculture in the People\'s Republic of China">agriculture</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-selflink selflink">space</a></li></ul></li></ul></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:History_of_science_and_technology_in_China" title="Template:History of science and technology in China"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:History_of_science_and_technology_in_China" title="Template talk:History of science and technology in China"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_science_and_technology_in_China&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DFH-1_Satellite.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2432" data-file-width="4320" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/DFH-1_Satellite.jpg/220px-DFH-1_Satellite.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/DFH-1_Satellite.jpg/330px-DFH-1_Satellite.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/DFH-1_Satellite.jpg/440px-DFH-1_Satellite.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DFH-1_Satellite.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Dong_Fang_Hong_I" title="Dong Fang Hong I">Dong Fang Hong I</a> - Chinese first satellite (1970), Space technology exhibition in Hohhot, Inner Mongolia, China</div></div></div>\n<p>After the United States threatened to use nuclear weapons during the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a>, Chairman <a href="/wiki/Mao_Zedong" title="Mao Zedong">Mao Zedong</a> decided that only a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_deterrent" title="Nuclear deterrent">nuclear deterrent</a> of its own would guarantee the security of the newly founded <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">PRC</a>. Additionally, he wanted China to gain status among the world\'s powers that&#8212;as he felt&#8212;did not respect him.  From this, he decided instead to only implement his new plan with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_of_China" title="Republic of China">Republic of China</a> (present-day Taiwan) as "China". Thus, Mao announced his decision to develop China\'s own strategic weapons, including nuclear bombs and associated missiles for the warheads, during a <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_China" title="Communist Party of China">Communist Party of China</a> (CPC) Central Committee meeting held on January 15, 1955. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_nuclear_weapons_program" title="Chinese nuclear weapons program">Chinese nuclear weapons program</a> was designated by the codename of "02".\n</p><p>The <a class="extiw" href="https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E5%9B%BD%E9%98%B2%E9%83%A8%E7%AC%AC%E4%BA%94%E7%A0%94%E7%A9%B6%E9%99%A2" title="zh:&#22269;&#38450;&#37096;&#31532;&#20116;&#30740;&#31350;&#38498;">Fifth Academy</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_National_Defense_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="Ministry of National Defense of the People\'s Republic of China">National Defense Ministry</a> (<span lang="zh-CN" title="Chinese language text">&#22269;&#38450;&#37096;&#31532;&#20116;&#30740;&#31350;&#38498;</span>) was founded on October 8, 1956, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tsien_Hsue-shen" title="Tsien Hsue-shen">Qian Xuesen</a>, who had just been deported from the United States after being accused of being a communist during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_Red_Scare" title="Second Red Scare">red scare</a>, as director. The Academy started the development of the first ballistic missile program, adopted on March 1, 1956 and known as the first <i>Twelve-Year-Plan for Chinese aerospace</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the launch of mankind\'s first artificial satellite, <a href="/wiki/Sputnik_1" title="Sputnik 1">Sputnik 1</a>, by the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> on October 4, 1957, Mao decided during the National Congress of the CPC on May 17, 1958 to make China an equal with the superpowers (&#8220;<span lang="zh-CN" title="Chinese language text">&#25105;&#20204;&#20063;&#35201;&#25630;&#20154;&#36896;&#21355;&#26143;</span>&#8221;) (We need to develop the artificial satellite too), by adopting <i>Project 581</i> with the objective of placing a satellite in orbit by 1959 to celebrate the 10th anniversary of the PRC\'s founding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>\nThis goal would be achieved in three phases: developing <a href="/wiki/Sounding_rocket" title="Sounding rocket">sounding rockets</a> first, then launching small satellites and in the final phase, large satellites.\n</p>\n<ul><li>The construction of China\'s first missile test base, code-named <a href="/wiki/Jiuquan_Satellite_Launch_Center" title="Jiuquan Satellite Launch Center">Base 20</a> (<span lang="zh-CN" title="Chinese language text">&#35199;&#21271;&#32508;&#21512;&#23548;&#24377;&#35797;&#39564;&#22522;&#22320;</span>), started in April 1958 and it entered service on October 20 of the same year.</li>\n<li>The first Chinese missile was built in October 1958 as a reverse-engineered copy of the Soviet <a href="/wiki/R-2_(missile)" title="R-2 (missile)">R-2</a> <a href="/wiki/Short-range_ballistic_missile" title="Short-range ballistic missile">short-range ballistic missile</a> (SRBM), itself an upgraded version of a <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">German</a> <a href="/wiki/V-2_rocket" title="V-2 rocket">V-2 rocket</a>. Its range was 590&#160;km, weighing 20.5 tons and propelled with liquid oxygen and alcohol.</li>\n<li>China\'s first ever <a href="/wiki/T-7_(rocket)" title="T-7 (rocket)">T-7</a> sounding rocket was successfully launched from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nanhui" title="Nanhui">Nanhui</a> launch site on February 19, 1960.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nhwsj.gov.cn_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-nhwsj.gov.cn-5">[5]</a></sup></li>\n<li>China started to develop <a href="/wiki/Medium-range_ballistic_missile" title="Medium-range ballistic missile">medium-range ballistic missiles</a> (MRBM) in July 1960, with an increased range double that of the R-2.</li></ul>\n<p>During the cordial Sino-Soviet relations of the 1950s, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USSR" title="USSR">USSR</a> engaged in a cooperative technology transfer program with the PRC under which they trained Chinese students and provided the fledgling program with a sample <a href="/wiki/R-2_(missile)" title="R-2 (missile)">R-2</a> rocket. But when Soviet premier <a href="/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev" title="Nikita Khrushchev">Nikita Khrushchev</a> was denounced as <a href="/wiki/Revisionism_(Marxism)" title="Revisionism (Marxism)">revisionist</a>, with Mao asserting that there had been a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Counter-revolution" title="Counter-revolution">counter-revolution</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> and that <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalism</a> had been restored, the friendly relationship between the two countries turned to confrontation. As a consequence, all Soviet technological assistance was abruptly withdrawn after the 1960 <a href="/wiki/Sino-Soviet_split" title="Sino-Soviet split">Sino-Soviet split</a>.\n</p><p>Only 17 days after the last Soviet expert had left China, the first Soviet built R-2 rocket fueled with Chinese-made propellant was launched with success on September 10, 1960. Due to Cold War tension, Mao decided in December 1963 that China should develop missile defence system capacity. During a conference held on February 2, 1964, <i>directive 640</i> (640&#25351;&#31034;)was adopted (later known as <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Project_640&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Project 640 (page does not exist)">Project 640</a>)</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>The first successful launch of a Chinese 1059 SRBM missile copy of the R-2 was conducted only two months later on November 5, 1960. The missile was also designated <a href="/wiki/Dongfeng_(missile)#Dongfeng_1_(SS-2)" title="Dongfeng (missile)">DF-1</a>. The first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DF-2" title="DF-2">DF-2</a> MRBM was tested on March 21, 1962, but failed.</li>\n<li>Development eventually continued with the redesigned <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=DF-2A&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="DF-2A (page does not exist)">DF-2A</a> MRBM which was successfully tested on June 29, 1964. It would enter service by the end of 1966.</li>\n<li>The first successful launch and recovery of a <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=T-7A(S1)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="T-7A(S1) (page does not exist)">T-7A(S1)</a> sounding rocket carrying a biological experiment (transporting eight white mice) was on July 19, 1964 from <a href="/wiki/Base_603" title="Base 603">Base 603</a> (&#23433;&#24509;&#24191;&#24503;&#35475;&#33410;&#28193;&#20013;&#22269;&#31185;&#23398;&#38498;&#20845;&#12295;&#19977;&#22522;&#22320;.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></li>\n<li>China started to develop the <a href="/wiki/DF-5" title="DF-5">DF-5</a> <a href="/wiki/Intercontinental_ballistic_missile" title="Intercontinental ballistic missile">intercontinental ballistic missile</a> (ICBM) program in August 1965. It was designed to carry a single nuclear warhead and has a maximum range of 12000&#160;km. In November 1966, it was decided to build a second ballistic missile test site, the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_Missile_Test_Site&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Northern Missile Test Site (page does not exist)">Northern Missile Test Site</a> (&#21326;&#21271;&#23548;&#24377;&#35797;&#39564;&#22330;)) in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shanxi_Province" title="Shanxi Province">Shanxi Province</a>, farther away from China\'s northern border.</li>\n<li>On October 27, 1966, a nuclear-tipped DF-2A missile was launched from Jiuquan and the 20 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kiloton" title="Kiloton">kilotons</a> yield nuclear warhead exploded at the height of 569 meters over the target in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lop_Nor" title="Lop Nor">Lop Nor</a> or <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Lop_Nor_Base_21&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Lop Nor Base 21 (page does not exist)">Base 21</a> situated 894&#160;km away.</li>\n<li>On December 26, 1966, China tested its first indigenously developed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DF-3" title="DF-3">DF-3</a> <a href="/wiki/Intermediate-range_ballistic_missile" title="Intermediate-range ballistic missile">intermediate-range ballistic missile</a> (IRBM) with success. The DF-3 was a single-stage, single-warhead missile with a maximum range of 2500&#160;km. The development of the <a href="/wiki/DF-4" title="DF-4">DF-4</a> IRBM began in 1967 in parallel with the single-stage DF-3.</li>\n<li>In March 1967, development started on the <a href="/wiki/JL-1" title="JL-1">JL-1</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SLBM" title="SLBM">submarine-launched ballistic missile</a> to accompany the <a href="/wiki/Type_092_submarine" title="Type 092 submarine">Type 092</a> <a href="/wiki/Ballistic_missile_submarine" title="Ballistic missile submarine">ballistic missile submarine</a> (SSBN) also in development.</li></ul>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Launch_of_Long_March_3B_Rocket.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1423" data-file-width="1039" decoding="async" height="301" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/The_Launch_of_Long_March_3B_Rocket.jpg/220px-The_Launch_of_Long_March_3B_Rocket.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/The_Launch_of_Long_March_3B_Rocket.jpg/330px-The_Launch_of_Long_March_3B_Rocket.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/The_Launch_of_Long_March_3B_Rocket.jpg/440px-The_Launch_of_Long_March_3B_Rocket.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Launch_of_Long_March_3B_Rocket.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The launch of Long March 3B Rocket in 1997</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Long_March_2D_launching_VRSS-1_afar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2935" data-file-width="2103" decoding="async" height="307" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Long_March_2D_launching_VRSS-1_afar.jpg/220px-Long_March_2D_launching_VRSS-1_afar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Long_March_2D_launching_VRSS-1_afar.jpg/330px-Long_March_2D_launching_VRSS-1_afar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Long_March_2D_launching_VRSS-1_afar.jpg/440px-Long_March_2D_launching_VRSS-1_afar.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Long_March_2D_launching_VRSS-1_afar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Long_March_2D" title="Long March 2D">Long March 2D</a> lifting off in 2012</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Long_March_2D_launching_off_pad_with_VRSS-1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4927" data-file-width="3355" decoding="async" height="323" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Long_March_2D_launching_off_pad_with_VRSS-1.jpg/220px-Long_March_2D_launching_off_pad_with_VRSS-1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Long_March_2D_launching_off_pad_with_VRSS-1.jpg/330px-Long_March_2D_launching_off_pad_with_VRSS-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Long_March_2D_launching_off_pad_with_VRSS-1.jpg/440px-Long_March_2D_launching_off_pad_with_VRSS-1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Long_March_2D_launching_off_pad_with_VRSS-1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Jiuquan_Satellite_Launch_Center" title="Jiuquan Satellite Launch Center">Jiuquan Satellite Launch Center</a>, one of the first Chinese spaceport</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_EVA_spacesuit_(2).JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1050" data-file-width="1400" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Chinese_EVA_spacesuit_%282%29.JPG/220px-Chinese_EVA_spacesuit_%282%29.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Chinese_EVA_spacesuit_%282%29.JPG/330px-Chinese_EVA_spacesuit_%282%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Chinese_EVA_spacesuit_%282%29.JPG/440px-Chinese_EVA_spacesuit_%282%29.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_EVA_spacesuit_(2).JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chinese EVA spacesuit "Feitian".</div></div></div>\n<p>As the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_race" title="Space race">space race</a> between the two superpowers reached its climax with the conquest of the Moon, Mao and <a href="/wiki/Zhou_Enlai" title="Zhou Enlai">Zhou Enlai</a> decided on July 14, 1967 that the PRC should not be left behind, and started China\'s own crewed space program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>China\'s first spacecraft designed for human occupancy was named <i>Shuguang-1</i> (&#26329;&#20809;&#19968;&#21495;) in January 1968.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=China%27s_Space_Medical_Institute&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="China\'s Space Medical Institute (page does not exist)">China\'s Space Medical Institute</a> (&#33322;&#22825;&#21307;&#23398;&#24037;&#31243;&#30740;&#31350;&#25152;) was founded on April 1, 1968, and the <a href="/wiki/Central_Military_Commission" title="Central Military Commission">Central Military Commission</a> issued the order to start the selection of astronauts.</li></ul>\n<ul><li>As part of the "third line" effort to relocate critical defense infrastructure to the relatively remote interior (away from the Soviet border), it was decided to construct a new space center in the mountainous region of <a href="/wiki/Xichang" title="Xichang">Xichang</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Sichuan" title="Sichuan">Sichuan</a> province, code-named <a href="/wiki/Xichang_Satellite_Launch_Center" title="Xichang Satellite Launch Center">Base 27</a>.</li>\n<li>A first liquid-propellant DF-3 medium-range ballistic missile was successfully launched from the Northern Missile Test Site on December 18, 1968, inaugurating the test site.</li>\n<li>In August 1969, the development of China\'s first heavy-lift <a href="/wiki/Launch_vehicle" title="Launch vehicle">satellite launch vehicle</a> (SLV), the <a href="/wiki/Feng_Bao_1" title="Feng Bao 1">Feng Bao 1</a> (FB-1) (&#39118;&#26292;&#19968;&#21495;, was started by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Shanghai%E2%80%99s_2nd_Bureau_of_Mechanic-Electrical_Industry&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Shanghai&#8217;s 2nd Bureau of Mechanic-Electrical Industry (page does not exist)">Shanghai&#8217;s 2nd Bureau of Mechanic-Electrical Industry</a>. The all-liquid two-stage launcher was derived from the DF-5 ICBM. Only a few months later, a parallel heavy-lift SLV program, also based on the same DF-5 ICBM and known as <a href="/wiki/Long_March_2A" title="Long March 2A">CZ-2</a>, was started in Beijing by the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=First_Space_Academy&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="First Space Academy (page does not exist)">First Space Academy</a>.</li>\n<li>The DF-4 was used to develop the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long_March_rocket" title="Long March rocket">Long March-1 SLV</a>. A newly designed spin-up orbital insertion solid propellant rocket motor third stage was added to the two existing <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">Nitric acid</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UDMH" title="UDMH">UDMH</a> liquid propellant stages. An attempt to use this vehicle to launch a Chinese satellite before <a href="/wiki/Ohsumi_(satellite)" title="Ohsumi (satellite)">Japan\'s first attempt</a> ended in failure on November 16, 1969.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></li>\n<li>The first DF-4 liquid-propellant with two-stage, single-warhead IRBM was tested with success on January 30, 1970. The addition of a second-stage allowed the missile to increase its range to over 4750&#160;km.</li>\n<li>The second satellite launch attempt on April 24, 1970 was successful. A <a href="/wiki/Long_March_1" title="Long March 1">CZ-1</a> was used to launch the 173&#160;kg <i><a href="/wiki/Dong_Fang_Hong_I" title="Dong Fang Hong I">Dong Fang Hong I</a></i> (&#19996;&#26041;&#32418;&#19968;&#21495;, meaning <a href="/wiki/The_East_Is_Red_(song)" title="The East Is Red (song)">The East Is Red</a> I), also known as <i>Mao-1</i>. It was the heaviest first satellite placed into orbit by a nation, exceeding the combined masses of the first satellites of the other four previous countries. The third stage of the CZ-1 was specially equipped with a 40 m<sup>2</sup> solar reflector (&#35266;&#23519;&#29699;) deployed by the <a href="/wiki/Centrifugal_force" title="Centrifugal force">centrifugal force</a> developed by the spin up orbital insertion solid propellant stage. Therefore, the faint <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">magnitude</a> 5 to 8 brightness of the DFH-1 made the satellite (at best) barely visible with naked eyes was consequently dramatically increased to a comfortable magnitude 2 to 3.</li>\n<li>The PRC\'s second satellite was launched with the last of the CZ-1 SLVs on March 3, 1971. The 221&#160;kg <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=ShiJian-1&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="ShiJian-1 (page does not exist)">ShiJian-1</a> (SJ-1) was equipped with a <a href="/wiki/Magnetometer" title="Magnetometer">magnetometer</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic-ray" title="Cosmic-ray">cosmic-ray</a>/<a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">x-ray</a> detectors.</li>\n<li>The first crewed space program known as <i>Project 714</i>, was officially adopted in April 1971 with the goal of sending two astronauts into space by 1973 aboard the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shuguang_spacecraft" title="Shuguang spacecraft">Shuguang</a></i> spacecraft. The first screening process for astronauts had already ended on March 15, 1971, with 19 astronauts chosen. The program would soon be cancelled due to political turmoil.</li>\n<li>A first flight test of the DF-5 ICBM was carried out in October 1971.</li>\n<li>On August 10, 1972, the new heavy-lift SLV FB-1 made its maiden test flight, with only partial success.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (September 2013)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Long_March_2A" title="Long March 2A">CZ-2A</a> launcher, originally designed to carry the Shuguang-1 spacecraft, was first tested on November 5, 1974, carrying China&#8217;s first <a href="/wiki/Fanhui_Shi_Weixing" title="Fanhui Shi Weixing">FSW-0</a> recoverable satellite, but failed. After some redesign work, the modified <a href="/wiki/Long_March_2C" title="Long March 2C">CZ-2C</a> successfully launched the <a href="/wiki/Fanhui_Shi_Weixing" title="Fanhui Shi Weixing">FSW-0 No.1</a> recoverable satellite (&#36820;&#22238;&#24335;&#21355;&#26143;) into orbit on November 26, 1975.</li>\n<li>After expansion, the Northern Missile Test Site was upgraded as a test base in January 1976 to become the Northern Missile Test Base (&#21326;&#21271;&#23548;&#24377;&#35797;&#39564;&#22522;&#22320;) known as <a href="/wiki/Taiyuan_Satellite_Launch_Center" title="Taiyuan Satellite Launch Center">Base 25</a>.</li></ul>\n<p>After Mao died on September 9, 1976, his rival, <a href="/wiki/Deng_Xiaoping" title="Deng Xiaoping">Deng Xiaoping</a>, denounced during the <a href="/wiki/Cultural_Revolution" title="Cultural Revolution">Cultural Revolution</a> as <a href="/wiki/Reactionary" title="Reactionary">reactionary</a> and therefore forced to retire from all his offices, slowly re-emerged as China\'s new leader in 1978. At first, new development was slowed. Then, several key projects deemed unnecessary were simply cancelled&#8212;the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Fanji_ABM_system&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Fanji ABM system (page does not exist)">Fanji ABM system</a>, the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Xianfeng_Anti-Missile_Super_Gun&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Xianfeng Anti-Missile Super Gun (page does not exist)">Xianfeng Anti-Missile Super Gun</a>, the ICBM Early Warning Network <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=7010_Tracking_Radar&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="7010 Tracking Radar (page does not exist)">7010 Tracking Radar</a> and the land-based high-power anti-missile laser program. Nevertheless, some development did proceed.\n</p>\n<ul><li>The first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yuanwang" title="Yuanwang">Yuanwang</a>-class space tracking ship was commissioned in 1979.</li>\n<li>The first full-range test of the DF-5 ICBM was conducted on May 18, 1980. The payload reached its target located 9300&#160;km away in the South Pacific (<span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Chinese_space_program&amp;params=7_0_S_117_33_E_&amp;title=DF-5+ICBM+test+impact"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">7&#176;0&#8242;S</span> <span class="longitude">117&#176;33&#8242;E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">&#65279; / &#65279;</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">7.000&#176;S 117.550&#176;E</span><span style="display:none">&#65279; / <span class="geo">-7.000; 117.550</span></span><span style="display:none">&#65279; (<span class="fn org">DF-5 ICBM test impact</span>)</span></span></span></a></span>) and retrieved five minutes later by helicopter.</li>\n<li>Further development of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long_March_rocket" title="Long March rocket">Long March rocket</a> series allowed the PRC to initiate a commercial launch program in 1985, which has since launched over 30 foreign satellites, primarily for <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">European</a> and <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asian</a> interests.</li>\n<li>The next crewed space program was even more ambitious and proposed in March 1986, as <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Project_863&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Project 863 (page does not exist)">Astronautics plan 863-2</a></i>. This consisted of a crewed spacecraft (Project 863-204) used to ferry astronaut crews to a space station (Project 863-205). Several spaceplane designs were rejected two years later and a simpler space capsule was chosen instead. Although the project did not achieve its goals, it would ultimately evolve into the 1992 <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Project_921" title="Project 921">Project 921</a></i>.</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Aerospace_Industry" title="Ministry of Aerospace Industry">Ministry of Aerospace Industry</a> was founded on July 5, 1988.</li>\n<li>On September 15, 1988, a <a href="/wiki/JL-1" title="JL-1">JL-1</a> SLBM was launched from a <a href="/wiki/Type_092_submarine" title="Type 092 submarine">Type 092 submarine</a>. The maximum range of the SLBM is 2150&#160;km.</li></ul>\n<p>Along Deng\'s policy of capitalist reforms in the Chinese economy, Chinese culture also changed. Therefore, names used in the space program, previously all chosen from the revolutionary history of the PRC, were soon replaced with mystical-religious ones. Thus, new <i>Long March</i> carrier rockets were renamed <i>Divine arrow</i> (&#31070;&#31661;),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> spacecraft <i>Divine vessel</i> (&#31070;&#33311;),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> space plane <i>Divine dragon</i> (&#31070;&#40857;),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> land-based high-power laser <i>Divine light</i> (&#31070;&#20809;)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> and supercomputer <i>Divine might</i> (&#31070;&#23041;).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>In June 1993, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/China_Aerospace_Industry_Corporation" title="China Aerospace Industry Corporation">China Aerospace Industry Corporation</a> (National Space Bureau) was founded in Beijing.</li>\n<li>On February 15, 1996, during the flight of the first <a href="/wiki/Long_March_3B" title="Long March 3B">Long March 3B</a> heavy carrier rocket carrying <a href="/wiki/Intelsat_708" title="Intelsat 708">Intelsat 708</a>, the rocket veered off course immediately after clearing the launch platform, crashing 22 seconds later. It crashed 1.85&#160;km (1.15&#160;mi) away from the launch pad into a nearby mountain village.</li>\n<li>On the 50th anniversary of the PRC\'s founding, China launched the <a href="/wiki/Shenzhou_1" title="Shenzhou 1">Shenzhou 1</a> spacecraft on November 20, 1999 and recovered it after a flight of 21 hours. The country became the third country with a successful crewed space program by sending an astronaut into space aboard <a href="/wiki/Shenzhou_5" title="Shenzhou 5">Shenzhou 5</a> on October 15, 2003 for more than 21 hours.</li></ul>\n<p>China has since turned its focus to extraterrestrial exploration starting with the Moon. The first <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Lunar_Exploration_Program" title="Chinese Lunar Exploration Program">Chinese Lunar Exploration Program</a> un-crewed lunar orbiter <a href="/wiki/Chang%27e_1" title="Chang\'e 1">Chang\'e 1</a> was successfully launched on October 24, 2007, making China the fifth nation to successfully orbit the Moon.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chinese_space_program_and_the_international_community">Chinese space program and the international community</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_space_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Chinese space program and the international community">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dual-use_technologies_and_outer_space">Dual-use technologies and outer space</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_space_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Dual-use technologies and outer space">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The PRC is a member of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Committee_on_the_Peaceful_Uses_of_Outer_Space" title="United Nations Committee on the Peaceful Uses of Outer Space">United Nations Committee on the Peaceful Uses of Outer Space</a> and a signatory to all <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> treaties and conventions on space, with the exception of the unpopular 1979 <a href="/wiki/Moon_Treaty" title="Moon Treaty">Moon Treaty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The United States government has long been resistant to the use of PRC launch services by American industry due to concerns over alleged civilian technology transfer that could have dual-use military applications to countries such as <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> or <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, and announced an official embargo against the PRC in 2000.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Thus, financial retaliatory measures have been taken on many occasions against several Chinese space companies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chinese_exclusion_policy_of_NASA">Chinese exclusion policy of NASA</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_space_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Chinese exclusion policy of NASA">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_exclusion_policy_of_NASA" title="Chinese exclusion policy of NASA">Chinese exclusion policy of NASA</a></div>\n<p>Due to security concerns, all researchers from the U.S. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Aeronautics_and_Space_Administration" title="National Aeronautics and Space Administration">National Aeronautics and Space Administration</a> (NASA) are prohibited from working with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People\'s Republic of China">Chinese citizens</a> affiliated with a Chinese state enterprise or entity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardiannasa_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-guardiannasa-19">[19]</a></sup> In April 2011, the <a href="/wiki/112th_United_States_Congress" title="112th United States Congress">112th United States Congress</a> banned NASA from using its funds to host Chinese visitors at NASA facilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-justice1_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-justice1-20">[20]</a></sup> In March 2013, the U.S. Congress passed legislation barring Chinese nationals from entering NASA facilities without a waiver from NASA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardiannasa_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-guardiannasa-19">[19]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The history of the U.S. exclusion policy can be traced back in 1998 when a U.S. Congressional Commission led by <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Cox" title="Christopher Cox">Christopher Cox</a> discovered that the technical information that American companies provided China for its commercial satellite ended up improving Chinese intercontinental ballistic missile technology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-21">[21]</a></sup> This was further aggravated in 2007 when China blew up its space station in the <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">low Earth orbit</a> to test a ground-based <a href="/wiki/Anti-satellite_weapon" title="Anti-satellite weapon">anti-satellite (ASAT) missile</a>. The debris created by the explosion contributed to the <a href="/wiki/Space_debris" title="Space debris">space junk</a> that litter Earth\'s orbit, exposing American military space assets at the risk such as accidental collision.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-21">[21]</a></sup> The United States also fears the Chinese application of dual-use space technology for nefarious purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-22">[22]</a></sup> The U.S. imposed an embargo to the U.S. - China space cooperation throughout the 2000s and by 2011, a clause inserted by then-Congressman <a href="/wiki/Frank_Wolf_(politician)" title="Frank Wolf (politician)">Frank Wolf</a> in the 2011 U.S. federal budget forbids NASA from hosting or participating in a joint scientific activity with China.\n</p><p>The Chinese response to the exclusion policy involved its own space policy of opening up its space station to the outside world, welcoming scientists coming from all countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-22">[22]</a></sup> American scientists have also boycotted NASA conferences due to its rejection of Chinese nationals in these events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Organization">Organization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_space_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Organization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Initially the space program of the PRC was organized under the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Liberation_Army" title="People\'s Liberation Army">People\'s Liberation Army</a>, particularly the Second Artillery Corps. In the 1990s, however, the PRC reorganized the space program as part of a general reorganization of the defense industry to make it resemble Western defense procurement.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/China_National_Space_Administration" title="China National Space Administration">China National Space Administration</a>, an agency within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commission_of_Science,_Technology_and_Industry_for_National_Defense" title="Commission of Science, Technology and Industry for National Defense">Commission of Science, Technology and Industry for National Defense</a> currently headed by <a href="/wiki/Sun_Laiyan" title="Sun Laiyan">Sun Laiyan</a>, is now responsible for launches. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long_March_rocket" title="Long March rocket">Long March rocket</a> is produced by the <a href="/wiki/China_Academy_of_Launch_Vehicle_Technology" title="China Academy of Launch Vehicle Technology">China Academy of Launch Vehicle Technology</a>, and satellites are produced by the <a href="/wiki/China_Aerospace_Science_and_Technology_Corporation" title="China Aerospace Science and Technology Corporation">China Aerospace Science and Technology Corporation</a>. The latter organizations are <a href="/wiki/State-owned_enterprise" title="State-owned enterprise">state-owned enterprises</a>; however, it is the intent of the PRC government that they should not be actively state managed and that they should behave much as private companies would in the West.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Universities_and_institutes">Universities and institutes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_space_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Universities and institutes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The space program also has close links with:\n</p>\n<ul><li>College of Aerospace Science and Engineering, <a href="/wiki/National_University_of_Defense_Technology" title="National University of Defense Technology">National University of Defense Technology</a></li>\n<li>School of Astronautics, <a href="/wiki/Beihang_University" title="Beihang University">Beihang University</a></li>\n<li>School of Aerospace, <a href="/wiki/Tsinghua_University" title="Tsinghua University">Tsinghua University</a></li>\n<li>School of Astronautics, <a href="/wiki/Northwestern_Polytechnical_University" title="Northwestern Polytechnical University">Northwestern Polytechnical University</a></li>\n<li>School of Aeronautics and Astronautics, <a href="/wiki/Zhejiang_University" title="Zhejiang University">Zhejiang University</a></li>\n<li>Institute of Aerospace Science and Technology, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shanghai_Jiaotong_University" title="Shanghai Jiaotong University">Shanghai Jiaotong University</a></li>\n<li>College of Aeronautics, <a href="/wiki/Harbin_Institute_of_Technology" title="Harbin Institute of Technology">Harbin Institute of Technology</a></li>\n<li>School of Automation Science and Electrical Engineering, <a href="/wiki/Beihang_University" title="Beihang University">Beihang University</a></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Space_cities">Space cities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_space_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Space cities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Dongfeng_space_city&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Dongfeng space city (page does not exist)">Dongfeng space city</a> (&#19996;&#39118;&#33322;&#22825;&#22478;)&#65292;also known as <i>Base 20</i> (&#20108;&#21313;&#22522;&#22320;) or <i>Dongfeng base</i> (&#19996;&#39118;&#22522;&#22320;)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></li>\n<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Beijing_space_city&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Beijing space city (page does not exist)">Beijing space city</a> (&#21271;&#20140;&#33322;&#22825;&#22478;)</li>\n<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Wenchang_space_city&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Wenchang space city (page does not exist)">Wenchang space city</a> (&#25991;&#26124;&#33322;&#22825;&#22478;)</li>\n<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Shanghai_space_city&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Shanghai space city (page does not exist)">Shanghai space city</a> (&#19978;&#28023;&#33322;&#22825;&#22478;)</li>\n<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Yantai_space_city&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Yantai space city (page does not exist)">Yantai space city</a> (&#28895;&#21488;&#33322;&#22825;&#22478;)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></li>\n<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Guizhou_Aerospace_Industrial_Park&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Guizhou Aerospace Industrial Park (page does not exist)">Guizhou Aerospace Industrial Park</a> (&#36149;&#24030;&#33322;&#22825;&#39640;&#26032;&#25216;&#26415;&#20135;&#19994;&#22253;)&#65292;also known as <i>Base 061</i> (&#33322;&#22825;&#9675;&#20845;&#19968;&#22522;&#22320;), founded in 2002 after approval of <i>Project 863</i> for industrialization of aerospace research centers (&#8220;&#22269;&#23478;863&#35745;&#21010;&#25104;&#26524;&#20135;&#19994;&#21270;&#22522;&#22320;&#8221;).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Suborbital_launch_sites">Suborbital launch sites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_space_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Suborbital launch sites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nanhui" title="Nanhui">Nanhui</a> (&#21335;&#27719;&#21439;&#32769;&#28207;&#38215;&#19996;&#36827;&#26449;) First successful launch of a <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=T-7M&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="T-7M (page does not exist)">T-7M</a> sounding rocket on February 19, 1960.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nhwsj.gov.cn_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-nhwsj.gov.cn-5">[5]</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Base_603" title="Base 603">Base 603</a> (&#23433;&#24509;&#24191;&#24503;&#35475;&#33410;&#28193;&#20013;&#22269;&#31185;&#23398;&#38498;&#20845;&#12295;&#19977;&#22522;&#22320;) Also known as <i>Guangde Launch Site</i> (&#24191;&#24503;&#21457;&#23556;&#22330;).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> The first successful flight of a biological experimental <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=T-7A(S1)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="T-7A(S1) (page does not exist)">T-7A(S1)</a> sounding rocket transporting eight white mice was launched and recovered on July 19, 1964.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Satellite_launch_centers">Satellite launch centers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_space_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Satellite launch centers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The PRC operates 4 satellite launch centers:\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Jiuquan_Satellite_Launch_Center" title="Jiuquan Satellite Launch Center">Jiuquan Satellite Launch Center</a> (JSLC)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Xichang_Satellite_Launch_Center" title="Xichang Satellite Launch Center">Xichang Satellite Launch Center</a> (XSLC)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Taiyuan_Satellite_Launch_Center" title="Taiyuan Satellite Launch Center">Taiyuan Satellite Launch Center</a> (TSLC)</li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wenchang_Satellite_Launch_Center" title="Wenchang Satellite Launch Center">Wenchang Satellite Launch Center</a> (WSLC)</li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Monitoring_and_control_centers">Monitoring and control centers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_space_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Monitoring and control centers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beijing_Aerospace_Command_and_Control_Center" title="Beijing Aerospace Command and Control Center">Beijing Aerospace Command and Control Center</a> (BACCC)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Xi%27an_Satellite_Control_Center" title="Xi\'an Satellite Control Center">Xi\'an Satellite Control Center</a> (XSCC) also known as <i>Base 26</i>&#65288;&#20108;&#21313;&#20845;&#22522;&#22320;&#65289;</li>\n<li>Fleet of six <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yuanwang" title="Yuanwang">Yuanwang</a>-class space tracking ships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Data_relay_satellite" title="Data relay satellite">Data relay satellite</a> (&#25968;&#25454;&#20013;&#32487;&#21355;&#26143;) <a href="/wiki/Tianlian_I" title="Tianlian I">Tianlian I</a> (&#22825;&#38142;&#19968;&#21495;), specially developed to decrease the communication time between the <a href="/wiki/Shenzhou_7" title="Shenzhou 7">Shenzhou 7</a> spaceship and the ground; it will also improve the amount of data that can be transferred. The current orbit coverage of 12 percent will thus be increased to a total of about 60 percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Deep Space Tracking Network composed with radio antennas in <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kunming" title="Kunming">Kunming</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urumuqi" title="Urumuqi">Urumuqi</a>, forming a 3000&#160;km VLBI&#65288;&#29978;&#38271;&#22522;&#32447;&#24178;&#28041;&#65289;.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_tracking_stations">Domestic tracking stations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_space_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Domestic tracking stations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<ul><li>New integrated land-based space monitoring and control network stations, forming a large triangle with <a href="/wiki/Kashgar" title="Kashgar">Kashi</a> in the north-west of China, <a href="/wiki/Jiamusi" title="Jiamusi">Jiamusi</a> in the north-east and <a href="/wiki/Sanya" title="Sanya">Sanya</a> in the south.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Weinan Station</li>\n<li>Changchun Station</li>\n<li>Qingdao Station</li>\n<li>Zhanyi Station</li>\n<li>Nanhai Station</li>\n<li>Tianshan Station</li>\n<li>Xiamen Station</li>\n<li>Lushan Station</li>\n<li>Jiamusi Station</li>\n<li>Dongfeng Station</li>\n<li>Hetian Station</li></ul>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Overseas_tracking_stations">Overseas tracking stations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_space_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Overseas tracking stations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Karachi" title="Karachi">Karachi</a> Station,In Pakistan</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Tarawa" title="Tarawa">Tarawa</a> Station, Kiribati (dismantled in 2003<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tarawa-dismantled_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-tarawa-dismantled-35">[35]</a></sup>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Malindi" title="Malindi">Malindi</a> Station</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Swakopmund_tracking_station" title="Swakopmund tracking station">Swakopmund tracking station</a> in Namibia</li>\n<li>China  Satellite Launch and Tracking Control General tracking hub in <a href="/wiki/Neuqu%C3%A9n_Province" title="Neuqu&#233;n Province">Neuqu&#233;n Province</a>, <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-36">[36]</a></sup> <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Chinese_space_program&amp;params=38.193014_S_70.147581_W_"><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">38&#176;11&#8242;35&#8243;S</span> <span class="longitude">70&#176;08&#8242;51&#8243;W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">&#65279; / &#65279;</span><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">38.193014&#176;S 70.147581&#176;W</span><span style="display:none">&#65279; / <span class="geo">-38.193014; -70.147581</span></span></span></a></span></li></ul>\n<p>Plus shared space tracking facilities with France, Brazil, Sweden and Australia.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crewed_landing_sites">Crewed landing sites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_space_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Crewed landing sites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siziwang_Banner" title="Siziwang Banner">Siziwang Banner</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Crewed_spaceflight_programs">Crewed spaceflight programs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_space_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Crewed spaceflight programs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Project_714">Project 714</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_space_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Project 714">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Shuguang_(spacecraft)" title="Shuguang (spacecraft)">Shuguang (spacecraft)</a></div>\n<p>As the <a href="/wiki/Space_Race" title="Space Race">Space Race</a> between the two superpowers reached its climax with humans landing on the Moon, <a href="/wiki/Mao_Zedong" title="Mao Zedong">Mao Zedong</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zhou_Enlai" title="Zhou Enlai">Zhou Enlai</a> decided on July 14, 1967 that the PRC should not be left behind, and therefore initiated China\'s own crewed space program. The top-secret Project 714 aimed to put two people into space by 1973 with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shuguang_spacecraft" title="Shuguang spacecraft">Shuguang</a> spacecraft. Nineteen <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PLAAF" title="PLAAF">PLAAF</a> pilots were selected for this goal on March 1971. The Shuguang-1 spacecraft to be launched with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CZ-2A" title="CZ-2A">CZ-2A</a> rocket was designed to carry a crew of two. The program was officially cancelled on May 13, 1972 for economic reasons, though the internal politics of the <a href="/wiki/Cultural_Revolution" title="Cultural Revolution">Cultural Revolution</a> likely motivated the closure.\n</p><p>The short-lived second crewed program was based on the successful implementation of landing technology (third in the World after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USSR" title="USSR">USSR</a> and United States) by <a href="/wiki/Fanhui_Shi_Weixing" title="Fanhui Shi Weixing">FSW satellites</a>. It was announced a few times in 1978 with the open publishing of some details including photos, but then was abruptly canceled in 1980. It has been argued that the second crewed program was created solely for propaganda purposes, and was never intended to produce results.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Astronautix_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Astronautix-37">[37]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Project_863">Project 863</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_space_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Project 863">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>A new crewed space program was proposed by the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Chinese Academy of Sciences">Chinese Academy of Sciences</a> in March 1986, as <i>Astronautics plan 863-2</i>. This consisted of a crewed spacecraft (Project 863-204) used to ferry astronaut crews to a space station (Project 863-205). In September of that year, astronauts in training were presented by the Chinese media. The various proposed crewed spacecraft were mostly spaceplanes. Project 863 ultimately evolved into the 1992 <i>Project 921</i>.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Project_921_.28Shenzhou.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Project_921_(Shenzhou)">Project 921 (Shenzhou)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_space_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Project 921 (Shenzhou)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Spacecraft">Spacecraft</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_space_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Spacecraft">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Shenzhou_program" title="Shenzhou program">Shenzhou program</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ShenzhouVII.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="2136" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/ShenzhouVII.jpg/220px-ShenzhouVII.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/ShenzhouVII.jpg/330px-ShenzhouVII.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/ShenzhouVII.jpg/440px-ShenzhouVII.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ShenzhouVII.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Shenzhou_7" title="Shenzhou 7">Shenzhou 7</a> re-entry module</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wikitrip_to_MAI_museum_2016-02-02_108.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Wikitrip_to_MAI_museum_2016-02-02_108.JPG/220px-Wikitrip_to_MAI_museum_2016-02-02_108.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Wikitrip_to_MAI_museum_2016-02-02_108.JPG/330px-Wikitrip_to_MAI_museum_2016-02-02_108.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Wikitrip_to_MAI_museum_2016-02-02_108.JPG/440px-Wikitrip_to_MAI_museum_2016-02-02_108.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wikitrip_to_MAI_museum_2016-02-02_108.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A model of Shenzhou spacecraft</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1992, authorization and funding was given for the first phase of Project 921, which was a plan to launch a crewed spacecraft. The Shenzhou program had four uncrewed test flights and two crewed missions. The first one was <i><a href="/wiki/Shenzhou_1" title="Shenzhou 1">Shenzhou 1</a></i> on November 20, 1999. On January 9, 2001 <i><a href="/wiki/Shenzhou_2" title="Shenzhou 2">Shenzhou 2</a></i> launched carrying test animals. <i><a href="/wiki/Shenzhou_3" title="Shenzhou 3">Shenzhou 3</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Shenzhou_4" title="Shenzhou 4">Shenzhou 4</a></i> were launched in 2002, carrying test dummies. Following these was the successful <i><a href="/wiki/Shenzhou_5" title="Shenzhou 5">Shenzhou 5</a></i>, China\'s first crewed mission in space on October 15, 2003, which carried <a href="/wiki/Yang_Liwei" title="Yang Liwei">Yang Liwei</a> in orbit for 21 hours and made China the third nation to launch a human into orbit. <i><a href="/wiki/Shenzhou_6" title="Shenzhou 6">Shenzhou 6</a></i> followed two years later ending the first phase of the Project 921. Missions are launched on the <a href="/wiki/Long_March_2F" title="Long March 2F">Long March 2F</a> rocket from the <a href="/wiki/Jiuquan_Satellite_Launch_Center" title="Jiuquan Satellite Launch Center">Jiuquan Satellite Launch Center</a>. The <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=China_Crewed_Space_Engineering_Office&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="China Crewed Space Engineering Office (page does not exist)">China Crewed Space Engineering Office</a> provides engineering and administrative support for the crewed Shenzhou missions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Space_laboratory">Space laboratory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_space_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Space laboratory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Project_921-2" title="Project 921-2">Project 921-2</a></div>\n<p>The second phase of the Project 921 started with <i><a href="/wiki/Shenzhou_7" title="Shenzhou 7">Shenzhou 7</a></i>, China\'s first spacewalk mission. Then, two crewed missions were planned to the first Chinese space laboratory. The PRC initially designed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shenzhou_spacecraft" title="Shenzhou spacecraft">Shenzhou spacecraft</a> with docking technologies imported from Russia, therefore compatible with the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> (ISS). On September 29, 2011, China launched <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tiangong_1" title="Tiangong 1">Tiangong 1</a>. This target module is intended to be the first step to testing the technology required for a planned space station.\n</p><p>On October 31, 2011, a Long March 2F rocket lifted the <a href="/wiki/Shenzhou_8" title="Shenzhou 8">Shenzhou 8</a> uncrewed spacecraft which docked twice with the Tiangong 1 module. The <a href="/wiki/Shenzhou_9" title="Shenzhou 9">Shenzhou 9</a> craft took off on 16 June 2012 with a crew of 3. It successfully docked with the Tiangong-1 laboratory on 18 June 2012, at 06:07 UTC, marking China\'s first manned spacecraft docking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Another manned mission, <a href="/wiki/Shenzhou_10" title="Shenzhou 10">Shenzhou 10</a>, launched on 11 June 2013 . The Tiangong 1 target module is then expected to be deorbited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A second space lab, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tiangong_2" title="Tiangong 2">Tiangong 2</a>, launched on 15 September 2016, 22:04:09 (UTC+8).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The launch mass was 8,600 kg, with a length of 10.4m and a width of 3.35m, much like the Tiangong 1. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Shenzhou_11" title="Shenzhou 11">Shenzhou 11</a> launched and rendezvoused with Tiangong 2 in October 2016, with an unconfirmed further mission Shenzhou 12 in the future. The Tiangong 2 brings with it the POLAR gamma ray burst detector, a space-Earth quantum key distribution and laser communications experiment to be used in conjunction with the Mozi &#8216;Quantum Science Satellite&#8217;, a liquid bridge thermocapillary convection experiment and a space material experiment. Also included is a stereoscopic microwave altimeter, a space plant growth experiment, and a multi-angle wide-spectral imager and multi-spectral limb imaging spectrometer. Onboard TG-2 there will also be the world&#8217;s first-ever in-space cold atomic fountain clock. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Space_station">Space station</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_space_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Space station">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_space_station" title="Chinese space station">Chinese space station</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tiangong_program" title="Tiangong program">Tiangong program</a></div>\n<p>A larger basic permanent space station (&#22522;&#26412;&#22411;&#31354;&#38388;&#31449;) would be the third and last phase of Project 921. This will be a modular design with an eventual weight of around 60 tons, to be completed sometime before 2020. The first section, designated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tiangong_3" title="Tiangong 3">Tiangong 3</a>, is scheduled for launch after Tiangong 2.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sdc20110307_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-sdc20110307-44">[44]</a></sup> Tiangong 3 will weigh 22 tons and be 18.1 metres long. Additional modules will be connected over several missions to build the space station.\n</p><p>This could also be the beginning of China\'s crewed international cooperation, the existence of which was officially disclosed for the first time after the launch of <i>Shenzhou 7</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Chinese space station is scheduled to be completed in 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Proposed_lunar_exploration">Proposed lunar exploration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_space_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Proposed lunar exploration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Lunar_Exploration_Program" title="Chinese Lunar Exploration Program">Chinese Lunar Exploration Program</a></div>\n<p>In February 2004, the PRC formally started the implementation phase of its uncrewed <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> exploration project. According to <a href="/wiki/Sun_Laiyan" title="Sun Laiyan">Sun Laiyan</a>, administrator of the <a href="/wiki/China_National_Space_Administration" title="China National Space Administration">China National Space Administration</a>, the project will involve three phases: orbiting the Moon; landing; and returning samples. The first phase planned to spend 1.4 billion <a href="/wiki/Renminbi" title="Renminbi">renminbi</a> (approx. US$170 million) to orbit a satellite around the Moon before 2007, which is ongoing. Phase two involves sending a lander before 2010. Phase three involves collecting lunar soil samples before 2020.\n</p><p>On November 27, 2005, the deputy commander of the crewed spaceflight program announced that the PRC planned to complete a space station and a crewed mission to the Moon by 2020, assuming funding was approved by the government.\n</p><p>On December 14, 2005, it was reported "an effort to launch lunar orbiting satellites will be supplanted in 2007 by a program aimed at accomplishing an uncrewed lunar landing. A program to return uncrewed space vehicles from the moon will begin in 2012 and last for five years, until the crewed program gets underway" in 2017, with a crewed Moon landing some time after that.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Nonetheless, the decision to develop a totally new <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">moon</a> rocket in the 1962 Soviet <a href="/wiki/Universal_Rocket#UR=700" title="Universal Rocket">UR-700M</a>-class (<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Aelita&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Project Aelita (page does not exist)">Project Aelita</a>) able to launch a 500-ton payload in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_transfer_orbit#Application_to_interplanetary_travel" title="Lunar transfer orbit">LTO</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (May 2011)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata"> &#8211; <a href="/wiki/Talk:Chinese_space_program#Dubious" title="Talk:Chinese space program">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup> and a more modest 50 tons LTO payload LV has been discussed in a 2006 conference by academician <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Zhang_Guitian&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Zhang Guitian (page does not exist)">Zhang Guitian</a> (&#24352;&#36149;&#30000;), a liquid propellant rocket engine specialist, who developed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CZ-2" title="CZ-2">CZ-2</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CZ-4A" title="CZ-4A">CZ-4A</a> rockets engines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-war-sky_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-war-sky-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChineseCrewedLunarRocket_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-ChineseCrewedLunarRocket-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zhangguitian_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-zhangguitian-50">[50]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On June 22, 2006, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Long_Lehao&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Long Lehao (page does not exist)">Long Lehao</a>, deputy chief architect of the lunar probe project, laid out a schedule for China\'s lunar exploration. He set 2024 as the date of China\'s first moonwalk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In September 2010, it was announced that the country is planning to carry out explorations in deep space by sending a man to the Moon by 2025. China also hopes to bring a moon rock sample back to Earth in 2017, and subsequently build an observatory on the Moon\'s surface. <a href="/wiki/Ye_Peijian" title="Ye Peijian">Ye Peijian</a>, Commander in Chief of the Chang&#8217;e programme and an academic at the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Chinese Academy of Sciences">Chinese Academy of Sciences</a>, added that China has the "full capacity to accomplish Mars exploration by 2013."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On January 3, 2019, Chang\'e 4, the China National Space Administration\'s lunar rover, successfully landed on the far side of the moon. The rover was able to transmit data back to Earth despite the lack of radio frequencies on the far side, via a dedicated satellite sent earlier to orbit the moon. The landing and data transmission is considered a landmark achievement for human space exploration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>\n</p><p><br/>\nOn December 14, 2013<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> China\'s <a href="/wiki/Chang%27e_3" title="Chang\'e 3">Chang\'e 3</a> became the first object to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soft_landing_(rocketry)" title="Soft landing (rocketry)">soft-land</a> on the Moon since <a href="/wiki/Luna_24" title="Luna 24">Luna 24</a> in 1976.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As indicated by the official <i>Chinese Lunar Exploration Program</i> insignia, denoted by a calligraphic Moon ideogram (&#26376;) in the shape of a nascent lunar crescent, with two human footsteps at its center, the ultimate objective of the program is to establish a permanent human presence on the Earth\'s natural satellite.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chang%27e-3_lunar_landing_site.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1245" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Chang%27e-3_lunar_landing_site.jpg/220px-Chang%27e-3_lunar_landing_site.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Chang%27e-3_lunar_landing_site.jpg/330px-Chang%27e-3_lunar_landing_site.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Chang%27e-3_lunar_landing_site.jpg/440px-Chang%27e-3_lunar_landing_site.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chang%27e-3_lunar_landing_site.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Annotated image of the approximate landing site of the Chinese Chang\'e-3 lander. It was launched at 17:30 UTC on 1 December 2013, and reached the Moon\'s surface on 14 December 2013. The lunar coordinates are: 44.12&#176;N 19.51&#176;W.</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Yang_Liwei" title="Yang Liwei">Yang Liwei</a> declared at the 16th Human in Space Symposium of International Academy of Astronautics (IAA) in Beijing, on May 22, 2007 that building a lunar base was a crucial step to realize a flight to Mars and farther planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to practice, since the whole project is only at a very early preparatory research phase, no official crewed Moon program has been announced yet by the authorities. But its existence is nonetheless revealed by regular intentional leaks in the media.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> A typical example is the <a href="/wiki/Chang%27e_3" title="Chang\'e 3">Lunar Roving Vehicle</a> (&#26376;&#29699;&#36710;) that was shown on a Chinese TV channel (&#19996;&#26041;&#21355;&#35270;) during the 2008 <a href="/wiki/International_Workers%27_Day" title="International Workers\' Day">May Day</a> celebrations.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_to_Mars_and_beyond">Mission to Mars and beyond</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_space_program&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Mission to Mars and beyond">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Yinghuo-1" title="Yinghuo-1">Yinghuo-1</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mars_Global_Remote_Sensing_Orbiter_and_Small_Rover" title="Mars Global Remote Sensing Orbiter and Small Rover">Mars Global Remote Sensing Orbiter and Small Rover</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Sun_Laiyan" title="Sun Laiyan">Sun Laiyan</a>, administrator of the <a href="/wiki/China_National_Space_Administration" title="China National Space Administration">China National Space Administration</a>, said on July 20, 2006 that China would start deep space exploration focusing on <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> over the next five years, during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eleventh_Five-Year_Plan_(People%27s_Republic_of_China)" title="Eleventh Five-Year Plan (People\'s Republic of China)">Eleventh Five-Year Plan</a> (2006&#8211;2010) Program period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first uncrewed Mars exploration program could take place sometime between now<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous.
